        <title>The genome evolution and domestication of tropical fruit mango | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Mango is one of the world&#8217;s most important tropical fruits. It belongs to the family Anacardiaceae, which includes several other economically important species, notably cashew, sumac and pistachio from other genera. Many species in this family produce family-specific urushiols and related phenols, which can induce contact dermatitis. We generate a chromosome-scale genome assembly of mango, providing a reference genome for the Anacardiaceae family. Our results indicate the occurrence of a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in mango. Duplicated genes preferentially retained include photosynthetic, photorespiration, and lipid metabolic genes that may have provided adaptive advantages to sharp historical decreases in atmospheric carbon dioxide and global temperatures. A notable example of an extended gene family is the chalcone synthase (CHS) family of genes, and particular genes in this family show universally higher expression in peels than in flesh, likely for the biosynthesis of urushiols and related phenols. Genome resequencing reveals two distinct groups of mango varieties, with commercial varieties clustered with India germplasms and demonstrating allelic admixture, and indigenous varieties from Southeast Asia in the second group. Landraces indigenous in China formed distinct clades, and some showed admixture in genomes. Analysis of chromosome-scale mango genome sequences reveals photosynthesis and lipid metabolism are preferentially retained after a recent WGD event, and expansion of CHS genes is likely associated with urushiol biosynthesis in mango. Genome resequencing clarifies two groups of mango varieties, discovers allelic admixture in commercial varieties, and shows distinct genetic background of landraces."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Mango is one of the world&#8217;s most important tropical fruits. <script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

        (function(w, d) {
            
            
            
                window.Component = {};

                var scriptEl = d.createElement('script');
                return (!('noModule' in scriptEl) && 'onbeforeload' in scriptEl)
            }

            var headScripts = [
                {'src': '/static/js/polyfill-es5-bundle-974eb189f7.js', 'async': false},

                var scriptEl = d.createElement('script');
                scriptEl.src = script.src;
                scriptEl.async = script.async;
                if (script.module === true) {
                    scriptEl.type = "module";
                    if (catchNoModuleSupport()) {

                } else if (script.module === false) {
                    scriptEl.setAttribute('nomodule', true)
                }
                if (script.charset) {
                    scriptEl.setAttribute('charset', script.charset);
                }

                return scriptEl;
            }

            for (var i = 0; i < headScripts.length; ++i) {
                var scriptEl = createScript(headScripts[i]);
                currentScript.parentNode.insertBefore(scriptEl, currentScript.nextSibling);
            }

    <script>
        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);
                }
            };

            var disclaimer = document.querySelector('.c-disclaimer[aria-hidden="false"]');
            window.dataLayer.push({event:'OneTrustGroupsUpdated'});
            if (disclaimer) {
                if (!elementInside(document.activeElement, disclaimer)) {
                    disclaimer.querySelector('button').focus();
                }
            } else {
                document.activeElement.blur();
            }
        }
    </script>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The genome evolution and domestication of tropical fruit mango"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Mango is one of the worlds most important tropical fruits. It belongs to the family Anacardiaceae, which includes several other economically important species, notably cashew, sumac and pistachio from other genera. Many species in this family produce family-specific urushiols and related phenols, which can induce contact dermatitis. We generate a chromosome-scale genome assembly of mango, providing a reference genome for the Anacardiaceae family. Our results indicate the occurrence of a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in mango. Duplicated genes preferentially retained include photosynthetic, photorespiration, and lipid metabolic genes that may have provided adaptive advantages to sharp historical decreases in atmospheric carbon dioxide and global temperatures. A notable example of an extended gene family is the chalcone synthase (CHS) family of genes, and particular genes in this family show universally higher expression in peels than in flesh, likely for the biosynthesis of urushiols and related phenols. Genome resequencing reveals two distinct groups of mango varieties, with commercial varieties clustered with India germplasms and demonstrating allelic admixture, and indigenous varieties from Southeast Asia in the second group. Landraces indigenous in China formed distinct clades, and some showed admixture in genomes. Analysis of chromosome-scale mango genome sequences reveals photosynthesis and lipid metabolism are preferentially retained after a recent WGD event, and expansion of CHS genes is likely associated with urushiol biosynthesis in mango. Genome resequencing clarifies two groups of mango varieties, discovers allelic admixture in commercial varieties, and shows distinct genetic background of landraces."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Mango is one of the worlds most important tropical fruits. It belongs to the family Anacardiaceae, which includes several other economically important species, notably cashew, sumac and pistachio from other genera. Many species in this family produce family-specific urushiols and related phenols, which can induce contact dermatitis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We generate a chromosome-scale genome assembly of mango, providing a reference genome for the Anacardiaceae family. Our results indicate the occurrence of a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in mango. Duplicated genes preferentially retained include photosynthetic, photorespiration, and lipid metabolic genes that may have provided adaptive advantages to sharp historical decreases in atmospheric carbon dioxide and global temperatures. A notable example of an extended gene family is the chalcone synthase (CHS) family of genes, and particular genes in this family show universally higher expression in peels than in flesh, likely for the biosynthesis of urushiols and related phenols. Genome resequencing reveals two distinct groups of mango varieties, with commercial varieties clustered with India germplasms and demonstrating allelic admixture, and indigenous varieties from Southeast Asia in the second group. Landraces indigenous in China formed distinct clades, and some showed admixture in genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Analysis of chromosome-scale mango genome sequences reveals photosynthesis and lipid metabolism are preferentially retained after a recent WGD event, and expansion of CHS genes is likely associated with urushiol biosynthesis in mango. Genome resequencing clarifies two groups of mango varieties, discovers allelic admixture in commercial varieties, and shows distinct genetic background of landraces.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Mango, commonly known as the king of fruits, is one of the most popular fruits in the world [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tharanathan RN, Yashoda HM, Prabha TN. Mango (Mangifera indica L.), the king of fruits-an overview. Food Reviews International. 2006;22:29." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6606e914">1</a>]. Mango is widely cultivated in tropical and warmer subtropical areas in the world. India, China, and Thailand are the top three producers. In 2016, the global production of mango was 46.5 million tons, which ranks as the fifth most produced fruit crop worldwide (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/">http://www.fao.org/faostat/</a>). Mango fruits are mainly consumed fresh, while some are processed into products like nectar, juice, jam, and powder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Tharanathan RN, Yashoda HM, Prabha TN. Mango (Mangifera indica L.), the king of fruits-an overview. Food Reviews International. 2006;22:29." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6606e921">1</a>]. The fruits demonstrate attractive visual appearance and offer a favorable sensory experience to consumers, making them growingly popular among world consumers. Nevertheless, like many other Anacardiaceae plants such as poison ivy, sumac, and cashew, mango produces phenolic compounds (e.g., urushiols) that can induce contact dermatitis, an undesired quality for fresh mango consumption [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schulze-Kaysers N, Feuereisen MM, Schieber A. Phenolic compounds in edible species of the anacardiaceae family - a review. RSC Adv. 2015;5:14." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6606e924">2</a>]. The biosynthetic pathways for these compounds remain largely uncharacterized but are believed to arise from initial polyketide synthase-like reactions mediated by chalcone synthase for phenolic ring formation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Weisberg AJ. Investigations into the molecular evolution of plant terpene, alkaloid, and urushiol biosynthetic enzymes. PhD thesis; 2014. p. 130." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6606e927">3</a>].</p><p>Mango is the member of the genus <i>Mangifera</i> in the Anacardiaceae family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wannan BS: Analysis of generic relationships in Anacardiaceae. Blumea  Biodiversity Evol Biogeography Plants 2006, 5:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6606e936">4</a>]. Within this genus, most, if not all, cultivated mangoes belong to the species <i>Mangifera indica</i>, although dozens of other <i>Mangifera</i> species produce edible fruits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kostermans AJGH, Bompard JM. The mangoes, their botany, nomenclature, horticulture and utilisation. London: Academic Press; 1993." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6606e945">5</a>]. It has a domestication history of over 4000years within a large area in the Indo-Burmese and Southeast Asia regions but spreads to other parts of the world since the fourteenth century [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mehrotra RC, Dilcher DL, Awasthi N. A palaeogene Mangifera-like leaf fossil from India. Phytomorphology. 1998;48:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6606e949">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sawangchote P, Grote PJ, Dilcher DL. Tertiary leaf fossils of Mangifera (Anacardiaceae) from Li Basin, Thailand as examples of the utility of leaf marginal venation characters. Am J Bot. 2009;96:204861." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6606e952">7</a>]. Traditional varieties have largely been produced through vegetative propagation by grafting of mutated branches, while mango cross-breeding has become dominant since its introduction to the USA, Australia, and China, producing a number of cultivars that have established world popularity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Knight RJ Jr, Schnell RJ. Mango introduction in Florida and the Haden cultivars significance to the modern industry. Econ Bot. 1994;48:7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6606e955">8</a>]. However, considering its long cultivation history and complex genetic backgrounds, it is still largely unknown if there are varieties that can serve as genetic resources different from germplasms currently preserved and produced.</p><p>Despite the availability of cytogenetics data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mukherjee SK. Mango: its allopolyploid nature. Nature. 1950;166:1967." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6606e961">9</a>], transcriptome data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Tafolla-Arellano JC, Zheng Y, Sun H, Jiao C, Ruiz-May E, Hernandez-Onate MA, Gonzalez-Leon A, Baez-Sanudo R, Fei Z, Domozych D, et al. Transcriptome analysis of mango (Mangifera indica L.) fruit epidermal peel to identify putative cuticle-associated genes. Sci Rep. 2017;7:46163." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6606e964">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sivankalyani V, Sela N, Feygenberg O, Zemach H, Maurer D, Alkan N. Transcriptome dynamics in mango fruit peel reveals mechanisms of chilling stress. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1579." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6606e967">11</a>], and genetic maps [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Luo C, Shu B, Yao Q, Wu H, Xu W, Wang S. Construction of a high-density genetic map based on large-scale marker development in mango using specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing (SLAF-seq). Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1310." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6606e970">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kuhn DN, Bally ISE, Dillon NL, Innes D, Groh AM, Rahaman J, Ophir R, Cohen Y, Sherman A. Genetic map of mango: a tool for mango breeding. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:577." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6606e973">13</a>], whole-genome resources for mangoes are still publically unavailable, which creates difficulties in genomic-based trait improvement and understanding of specialized Anacardiaceae biochemistry underneath. To conquer this, we sequenced and assembled the chromosome-level genome of mangoes. Our analysis reveals that the genome is highly heterozygous, and it has experienced extensive evolution and domestication which may lead to uniqueness and diversity of mango qualities.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Prior to deep sequencing, a genomic survey of 22 mango varieties with represented genetic background uncovered universally high levels of genome heterozygosity (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM3">3</a>: Table S1). Among them, the genome of the variety Alphonso, a traditional Indian cultivar, demonstrated a relatively low heterogeneity rate and was thus chosen for whole-genome sequencing and de novo assembly (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM1">1</a>: Supplementary Notes). The genome was de novo assembled based on single-molecule subreads generated by PacBio Sequel II platform, improved by a combination of paired-end and mate-paired short reads, and incorporated with Hi-C sequencing for scaffolding (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM4">4</a>: Table S2). The assembled scaffolds were further anchored to genetic maps with 20 linkage groups (pseudomolecules). The final assembly consists of 252 scaffolds, which spans 392.9Mb in total (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Tab1">1</a>), with the scaffold N50 size of 17.6Mb and contig N50 size of 3.5Mb, and with 90.1% anchoring to the linkage groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Luo C, Shu B, Yao Q, Wu H, Xu W, Wang S. Construction of a high-density genetic map based on large-scale marker development in mango using specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing (SLAF-seq). Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1310." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1000">12</a>], including 20 pseudomolecules with sizes ranging from 12.2 to 29.4Mb (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM5">5</a>: Table S3 and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM1">1</a>: Supplementary Notes); 391.7Mb of 392.6Mb (99.8%) was covered by &gt;50 PacBio subreads. Ninety-eight percent of the assembled RNA-Seq transcripts were mapped with single scaffolds with aligned length longer than 80% (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM6">6</a>: Table S4). Of the 6594 genetic markers from published linkage map [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Luo C, Shu B, Yao Q, Wu H, Xu W, Wang S. Construction of a high-density genetic map based on large-scale marker development in mango using specific-locus amplified fragment sequencing (SLAF-seq). Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1310." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1013">12</a>], 6543 (99.2%) were reliably detected in the assembly, and the majority of adjacent markers were located within a short distance in genome sequence assembly (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S1), confirming that the order and orientation of the scaffolds are largely correct. Furthermore, 95.1% of the ultra-conserved core eukaryotic genes based on Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA) analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:10617." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1019">14</a>] and 95.9% of the single-copy orthologs based on the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:32102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1023">15</a>] could be completely detected in the assembly, further confirming the continuity and quality of the assembled genome.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the mango genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Together, we generated a highly contiguous assembly of the heterozygous mango genome. Repetitive sequences account for 40.5% of the mango genome, of which 39.9% was annotated as retrotransposons (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig1">1</a>; Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM7">7</a>: Table S5). After masking the repetitive sequences, we carried out a combination of de novo gene prediction, homology comparison, and transcriptome-based annotation, as well as quality control to annotate coding genes on the genome (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM1">1</a>: Supplementary Notes). In total, 41,251 protein-coding genes were annotated (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM8">8</a>: Table S6, Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM9">9</a>: Table S7, and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2), of which 37,424 (90.7%) could be located onto the 20 pseudomolecules. In addition, a total of 599 tRNAs, 459 microRNAs, 560 small nuclear RNAs, and 275 ribosomal RNAs were identified in the mango genome.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Overview of the mango (<i>Mangifera indica</i>) genome assembly. The outer layer of colored blocks is a circular representation of the 20 pseudomolecules, with thick mark labeling each 5Mb. The distribution of genetic markers mapped to mango chromosomes is shown in (A). Repeat density (B) and gene density (C) are calculated in 100-kb windows sliding in 10-kb steps. Tandem duplicated genes are displayed in (D). Genes involved in disease resistance (E), pigment-related metabolisms (F, yellow lines represent carotenoid synthesis genes, green lines represent chl metabolism genes and red lines represent anthocyanin synthesis genes), lipid metabolism (G, red lines represent genes participant in the synthesis of triacylglycerol, sphingolipid, phospholipid and mitochondrial lipopolysaccharide, phospholipid signaling, and lipid trafficking; the green lines represent the rest lipid metabolism genes) and photosynthesis related genes (H, red lines represent photosystem genes, black lines represent the Calvin cycle genes, and green lines represent genes participant in sucrose and starch synthesis, glycolysis, and Krebs cycle) are also displayed. Transcription factors are shown in (I). The innermost layer shows inter-chromosomal synteny, with the red links representing syntenic blocks retained after a recent WGD in mango genome, and the gray links representing homologs as results of older WGD</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome evolution</h3><p>To investigate the evolutionary history of mango genome, we performed a gene family clustering using mango and 11 other representative angiosperm species, including 2 species in the same order Sapindales (<i>Citrus sinensis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Xu Q, Chen LL, Ruan X, Chen D, Zhu A, Chen C, Bertrand D, Jiao WB, Hao BH, Lyon MP, et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat Genet. 2013;45:5966." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1135">16</a>] (sweet orange) and <i>Dimocarpus longan</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lin Y, Min J, Lai R, Wu Z, Chen Y, Yu L, Cheng C, Jin Y, Tian Q, Liu Q, et al. Genome-wide sequencing of longan (Dimocarpus longan Lour.) provides insights into molecular basis of its polyphenol-rich characteristics. Gigascience. 2017;6:114." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1141">17</a>] (longan)), 7 in the same Eudicot clade (<i>Carica papaya</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ming R, Hou S, Feng Y, Yu Q, Dionne-Laporte A, Saw JH, Senin P, Wang W, Ly BV, Lewis KL, et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature. 2008;452:9916." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1148">18</a>], <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Arabidopsis Genome I. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature. 2000;408:796815." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1154">19</a>], <i>Theobroma cacao</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345:11814." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1160">20</a>], <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Guo S, Zhang J, Sun H, Salse J, Lucas WJ, Zhang H, Zheng Y, Mao L, Ren Y, Wang Z, et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat Genet. 2013;45:518." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1167">21</a>], <i>Prunus persica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Peach Genome I, Verde I, Abbott AG, Scalabrin S, Jung S, Shu S, Marroni F, Zhebentyayeva T, Dettori MT, Grimwood J, et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat Genet. 2013;45:48794." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1173">22</a>], <i>Vitis vinifera</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jaillon O, Aury JM, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A, Choisne N, Aubourg S, Vitulo N, Jubin C, et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature. 2007;449:4637." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1179">23</a>], and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tomato Genome C. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature. 2012;485:63541." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1186">24</a>]), and 2 outgroup species (<i>Oryza sativa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kawahara Y, de la Bastide M, Hamilton JP, Kanamori H, McCombie WR, Ouyang S, Schwartz DC, Tanaka T, Wu J, Zhou S, et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice (N Y). 2013;6:4." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1192">25</a>] and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Amborella Genome P. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1198">26</a>]) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM10">10</a>: Table S8). From the result, 248 single-copy families were used for phylogenetic tree construction and species divergence time estimation, which placed mango as a sister species in adjacent with sweet orange and longan, which is consistent with published results [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bell CD, Soltis DE, Soltis PS. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am J Bot. 2010;97:1296303." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1205">27</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig2">2</a>a). We estimated that mangoes diverged from the ancestor of longan and sweet orange ~70 million years ago (MYA).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="515" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>A recent whole genome duplication and resulted gene fate in mango genome. (<b>a</b>) Inferred phylogenetic tree across 12 plant species including mango. Estimated WGD events wereindicated with circles. (<b>b</b>) Frequency distributions of synonymous substitution rates (Ks) betweenhomologous gene pairs in syntenic blocks of mango-mango, mango-orange, mango-longan andorange-longan. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of expressional correlation coefficient of syntenic homologousgene pairs retained from recent WGD and ancient WGD in mango genome. (<b>d</b>) Enrichment ofmetabolic genes retained after the recent WGD of mango genome. The vertical dashed linerepresents average percentage of genes retained after the recent WGD</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To detect whole-genome duplications (WGD) in mangoes, we performed a genomic synteny analysis based on the self-comparison of mango coding genes, which identified 41 large collinear blocks with at least 50 homologous gene pairs. Among them, 38 collinear blocks have similar (0.30.4) median synonymous substitutions values (<i>Ks</i>) for homologous gene pairs (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM11">11</a>: Table S9). These collinear blocks were distributed across the 20 pseudochromosomes, spanning 50.7% length of chromosomes (181.3Mb/357.7Mb) and covering 62.1% of protein-coding genes (23,260/37,424) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM12">12</a>: Table S10), which strongly supports a recent WGD event in mango genome. An exemplary illustration of co-synteny is shown between chr. 3 and 7 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>e). In contrast to the findings of biased fractionation following WGD in maize [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Woodhouse MR, Schnable JC, Pedersen BS, Lyons E, Lisch D, Subramaniam S, Freeling M. Following tetraploidy in maize, a short deletion mechanism removed genes preferentially from one of the two homologs. PLoS Biol. 2010;8:e1000409." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1246">28</a>] or <i>Arabidopsis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Thomas BC, Pedersen B, Freeling M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 2006;16:93446." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1253">29</a>], there was no apparent difference for region lengths or gene density between the mango collinear blocks (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM11">11</a>: Table S9), indicating that the mango-specific WGD might be an auto-diploidization event. The calculation of <i>Ks</i> for mango vs. orange, mango vs. longan, and orange vs. longan collinear orthologs indicated that the WGD event occurred after the split of mango lineage and the ancestor of orange and longan (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig2">2</a>b); no WGD event was detected after the split in either genome of sweet orange and longan (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S3). This WGD event in the mango genome might date back to ~33MYA by mapping the WGD event onto the phylogeny.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="444" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Expansion of genes in metabolism of flavonoids, lipids, chlorophylls and terpenoids. (<b>a</b>) Gene family analysis showing expansion of genes in flavonoid biosynthesis, lipid metabolism, chlorophyll metabolism and isoprenoid biosynthesis, including CHS family. Numbers showing in cells represent genes counting of each family in each species. (<b>b</b>) Gene structure of a typical CHS peptide (here shows mango gene Mi07g07250). The blue and red blocks represent two conserved domains PF00195.15 and PF02797.11, respectively, and the red circles suggest three conserved residues, Cysteine, Histidine and Asparagine, respectively, within the sequence. (<b>c</b>) Topology of phylogenetic tree of genes containing conserved domains PF00195.15 and PF02797.11 from mango, sweet orange and Arabidopsis. CHS-encoding genes are classified in a distinctive class, here named CHS. (<b>d</b>) The expression pattern of <i>CHS</i> genes in mango cultivars. Unexpressed genes were not showed in this figure. (<b>e</b>) Macrosynteny and microsynteny among mango chr.3, orange chr. 9 and mango chr. 7. Top: macrosynteny patterns among mango chr.3, orange chr. 9 and mango chr. 7. Co-synteny regions are linked with grey belts, except synteny of the regions harboring <i>CHS</i> genes, which are highlighted with a red belt. Bottom: microsynteny of the regions harboring CHS genes among the three chr. The CHS genes are represented by triangles, and are linked with red lines</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Within the 5369 homologous gene pairs retained post-WGD, 508 gene pairs are transcriptional factors, accounting for 9.6% of the retained genes, more than the average level in the genome (5.6%, 2342/41,251). Consistently, Gene Ontology analysis revealed the gene pairs retained post-WGD are enriched in the DNA binding category of molecular function (GO:0003677, DNA binding, <i>p</i>=4.42e13) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM13">13</a>: Table S11). These results suggest the preferred retaining of essential and regulatory genes after WGD in the mango genome, consistent with the dosage hypothesis that genes with large number partners are preferred to be retained after WGD [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Birchler JA, Veitia RA. The gene balance hypothesis: from classical genetics to modern genomics. Plant Cell. 2007;19:395402." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1308">30</a>]. In addition, the expressional similarities analysis on multiple tissues found that the gene pairs retained from recent WGD event were significantly greater than those retained from ancient WGD (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM14">14</a>: Table S12 and Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig2">2</a>c). The omega values (<i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i>) for most of collinear homologous gene pairs were smaller than 1 (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S4), indicating that protein neofunctionalization may not be the predominant status for the retained genes from recent WGD.</p><p>Mango-specific recent WGD may also impact the different metabolism categories with distinct contributions. For instance, the percentage of retained gene from recent WGD in energy metabolism, glycan biosynthesis and metabolism, carbohydrate metabolism, and lipid metabolism was about 48.453.8%, much greater than the genome average 30.1% (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig2">2</a>d). Notably, genes involved in photosynthesis were preferentially retained after recent WGD (53.6%), especially for genes that participated in the Calvin cycle (56.1%) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM15">15</a>: Table S13). Half of the sugar metabolism-related genes were the results of recent WGD, and the majority of pathway members in sucrose synthesis (7/10) and starch synthesis (8/9) have recent WGD-retained duplicated genes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM16">16</a>: Table S14). These findings indicated recent WGD-retained genes may preferentially take participant in the assimilation, storage, and utilization of CO<sub>2</sub>. Lipid genes were also found to be preferentially retained post-WGD (47.9% vs. 30.1% in general). Interestingly, genes of lipid-saccharide conjugate biosynthesis were preferentially retained, including genes in galactolipid, sphingolipid, and liposaccharide metabolisms. In addition, genes related to lipid trafficking and phospholipid and triacylglycerol biosynthesis were preferentially retained in the mango genome (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM17">17</a>: Table S15). Isoprenoid synthesis genes are also preferentially retained, especially for the genes involved in synthesis of precursor isopentenyl diphosphates (IPP), that is, most of the genes in the cytosolic mevalonate (MVA) pathway (4/6) and plastidic 2-<i>C</i>-methyl-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-erythritol-4-phosphate (MEP) pathway (6/7) have duplicated copies retained from recent WGD (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM18">18</a>: Table S16).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene expansion</h3><p>Gene family analysis among 12 plant species provided hints for a better understanding of the genetic basis of mango biology. Among the 3791 gene families with at least 20 members across 12 plants, we detected expansion of 2249 gene families and contraction of 491 gene families in the mango genome (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig2">2</a>a and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM19">19</a>: Table S17). High ratio of gene expansion to contraction (4.5) in the mango genome is in striking contrast to sweet orange (0.6) and longan (0.4). Among the 12 plants we investigated, other species with recent WGD events (&lt;80MYA), including <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice, and tomato, also demonstrated higher expansion/contraction ratio than other species, but the highest ratio was in mango genome, reflecting a relatively recent occurrence of the WGD event in the mango genome. Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis showed that the significantly expanded gene families in mangoes are enriched in basic biological functions, primarily in phosphorylation process and nucleotide-binding function; also enriched are genes in response to biotic stimulus response (<i>p</i>&lt;1e15) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM20">20</a>: Table S18).</p><p>Considering that mango individuals suffer from diseases through all stages of their life cycle that result in rot, necrosis, spots, etc., we analyzed the gene composition and phylogeny of disease resistance-related gene families in the mango genome and compared them to the phylogenetic close plants. In total, we identified 437 genes encoding nucleotide-binding site and leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR), as well as 296 receptor-like genes and 13 genes of lipoxygenase. Among these gene families, receptor-like genes, NBS-LRR-encoded genes experienced expansion in mango genome compared to sweet orange genome (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM21">21</a>: Table S19).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expansion of CHS genes: relation to specialized phenolic biochemistry</h3><p>Among the expanded families, several mango gene families are involved in flavonoid biosynthesis, lipid metabolism, chlorophyll biosynthesis, and isoprenoid synthesis (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>a). Notable among this expansion is chalcone synthase gene (CHS) family. Our results show that the mango CHS gene family is composed of 23 members in the mango genome, whereas only 12 and 4 were identified in sweet orange and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>a). The CHS family members were confirmed by the identification of the genes containing conserved domains PF00195.18 and PF02797.14 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>b). Phylogenetic analysis showed that the genes in CHS family split into 2 independent groups, one of which clustered the majority of the mango CHS family members (19 of 23) together with a <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene that has been demonstrated to be a bona fide CHS gene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Abe I, Morita H. Structure and function of the chalcone synthase superfamily of plant type III polyketide synthases. Nat Prod Rep. 2010;27:80938." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1407">31</a>]; the remaining 4 mango genes were clustered with <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes encoding CHS-like (CHSL) hydroxyalkyl-pyrone synthases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kim SS, Grienenberger E, Lallemand B, Colpitts CC, Kim SY, Souza Cde A, Geoffroy P, Heintz D, Krahn D, Kaiser M, et al. LAP6/POLYKETIDE SYNTHASE A and LAP5/POLYKETIDE SYNTHASE B encode hydroxyalkyl alpha-pyrone synthases required for pollen development and sporopollenin biosynthesis in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant Cell. 2010;22:404566." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1413">32</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>c). Based on transcriptomic analyses, the expression of <i>CHS</i> genes were generally higher than that of CHSL members in mangoes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>d).</p><p>Mango produces urushiols and related anacardic acids, characterized by an aromatic ring linked to a hydrocarbon tail of differing chain lengths and unsaturation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schulze-Kaysers N, Feuereisen MM, Schieber A. Phenolic compounds in edible species of the anacardiaceae family - a review. RSC Adv. 2015;5:14." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1428">2</a>]. Urushiols are characteristic of many Anacardiaceae species like poison ivy, sumac, cashew, and mango. Current knowledge is limited on the mechanisms of urushiol biosynthesis, but the first committed step in the pathway is believed to be catalyzed by CHS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Weisberg AJ. Investigations into the molecular evolution of plant terpene, alkaloid, and urushiol biosynthetic enzymes. PhD thesis; 2014. p. 130." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1431">3</a>]. CHS also generates the aromatic ring of anthocyanins, the red fruit peel pigment found in many consumer-preferred mango varieties (e.g., Sensation and Alphonso) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ranganath KG, Shivashankara KS, Roy TK, Dinesh MR, Geetha GA, Pavithra KC, Ravishankar KV. Profiling of anthocyanins and carotenoids in fruit peel of different colored mango cultivars. J Food Sci Technol. 2018;55:456677." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1434">33</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>a and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S5). Expansion of <i>CHS</i> genes in mango genome highlights its important roles in the evolution of the Anacardiaceae clade. To shed light on roles of the <i>CHS</i> group in mangoes, we further analyzed the sequence, phylogeny, expression, and synteny characteristics of these genes in the mango genome.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="346" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Expression of genes in chlorophyll degradation and anthocyanin biosynthesis in mango fruits. (<b>a</b>) Demonstration of peel colors of three representative mango varieties. Peel of developing fruit of Hongyu is green, while that of ripe fruit is yellow; peel of Guire-82 keeps green even when ripen; peel of Sensation demonstrates red color. (<b>b</b>) Expression of genes in chlorophyll degradation pathway in peel and flesh of Hongyu fruits as well as peel of Guire-82. (<b>c</b>) Expression of genes in anthocyanin biosynthesis in peel and flesh of Sensation fruits, as well as peels of Hongyu and Guire-82</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phylogeny further split the CHS group genes into three clades, namely clades I, II, and III (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>c, Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6, Figure S7 and Figure S8). Three and two CHS genes are harbored in clades I and II, respectively. Only Mi14G0074300 and Mi14G0151200 had evident expressions, both showing remarkably higher expression in peel than in flesh in the red-peel varieties Alphonso and Sensation, while not significant expression was observed in mature fruits of yellow-peel variety Hongyu and green-peel variety Guire 82 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>d). Considering the red color of the mango peels is attributed to the accumulation of anthocyanins, the expression pattern suggests the role of CHSs in clades I and II in anthocyanin biosynthesis in mangoes. A majority of <i>CHS</i> genes (14 of 19) are in clade III, in which only one sweet orange homolog and one <i>Arabidopsis</i> homolog are present, indicating that the extensive expansion of the <i>CHS</i> genes mainly occurred among the clade III members in mangoes. We asked if the expansion is a widely occurring event in Anacardiaceae, and retrieved <i>CHS</i> homologs from genomes of pistachio (<i>Pistacia vera</i>) and <i>Sclerocarya birrea</i>, and those from assembled contigs of publically available transcriptomes of poison ivy (<i>Toxicodendron radicans</i>) and sumac (<i>Rhus chinensis</i>), two other Anacardiaceae species producing urushiols (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM3">3</a>: Table S20) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu P, Yang ZX, Chen XM, Yang P. RNA-Seq-based transcriptome and the reproduction-related genes for the aphid Schlechtendalia chinensis (Hemiptera, Aphididae). Genet Mol Res. 2017;16:gmr16019448." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1508">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Weisberg AJ, Kim G, Westwood JH, Jelesko JG. Sequencing and de novo assembly of the Toxicodendron radicans (poison ivy) transcriptome. Genes (Basel). 2017;8:317." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1511">35</a>]. Phylogeny clearly showed the contigs together with mango CHSs are clustered into three clades, and the majority of contigs are grouped with mango clade III genes, in agreement with mango peptide phylogeny (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S7 and Figure S8). The results suggested the extensive expansion of CHS was a commonly occurring event in Anacardiaceae, which was originated from a single <i>CHS</i> copy.</p><p>Many clade III genes (12 of 14) are located in two syntenic clusters in the mango genome, a result of the recent WGD, which is syntenic to a region in Chr. 9 of sweet orange genome with a single <i>CHS</i> gene (Cs9g11190), suggesting the WGD medicated gene expansion occurred after the split of Acanardiaceae and Rutaceae (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>e). Besides the regions harboring CHS genes, the co-synteny can be found among the majority of the chr. 3 and chr. 7 of mango and chr. 9 of sweet orange (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>e). Each cluster has several <i>CHS</i> genes, which are likely results from tandem duplications (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>e). Gene structure and sequence identity revealed that Mi03g0182300 and Mi07g0142900 show higher similarity with the sweet orange <i>CHS</i> gene (Cs9g11190), likely serving as ancestral genes of the tandem duplications (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM24">24</a>: Table S22). All the three sites key to the CHS activity are conserved among the mango CHS peptides (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S9).</p><p>Despite extensive duplications, class III <i>CHS</i> genes in early stages of tandem duplication tended to express highly compared to the products of later duplications (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>d). Only two genes showed evident expressions in fruits, and they exhibited ubiquitously higher expression in peels than in flesh of the varieties with different colors when ripe, indicating that the <i>CHS</i> genes in clade III are not involved in anthocyanin biosynthesis in mangoes. Moreover, urushiols are ubiquitously more abundant in peels than in flesh in different mango varieties, as there are abundant resin canals where urushiols are produced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Dunn IS, Liberato DJ, Castagnoli N Jr, Byers VS. Influence of chemical reactivity of urushiol-type haptens on sensitization and the induction of tolerance. Cell Immunol. 1986;97:18996." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1561">36</a>]. Ubiquitous higher expression of the clade III <i>CHS</i> genes in peels in different mango varieties suggest their roles in regulating biosynthesis of urushiols and related phenols in mangoes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Lopez-Cobo A, Verardo V, Diaz-de-Cerio E, Segura-Carretero A, Fernandez-Gutierrez A, Gomez-Caravaca AM. Use of HPLC- and GC-QTOF to determine hydrophilic and lipophilic phenols in mango fruit (Mangifera indica L.) and its by-products. Food Res Int. 2017;100:42334." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1568">37</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ferrer JL, Jez JM, Bowman ME, Dixon RA, Noel JP. Structure of chalcone synthase and the molecular basis of plant polyketide biosynthesis. Nat Struct Biol. 1999;6:77584." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1571">38</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig3">3</a>d).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Mango fruit peel pigmentation</h3><p>During the process of fruit development and ripening, mango fruit peels usually undergo dramatic, mild, or no color changes, resulting in combinations of yellow, red and green pigmentation that is mostly variety-dependent. For example, fruits of the variety Sensation undergo rapid color changes, resulting in light to dark red coloration of peels in the process of fruit development (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>a). Consistent with the long-term understanding that red color of fruits is a result of anthocyanin accumulation, most genes in the anthocyanin biosynthesis pathways exhibited tremendously higher expression levels in red-peel Sensation than in peels of red pigment-free Hongyu and Guire-82 fruits (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>b). We also observed remarkably higher expression levels of most anthocyanin biosynthesis genes in Sensation fruit peels than in flesh, consistent with the observations in the leaves that the light stimulated the synthesis of anthocyanin and the accumulated anthocyanin protect plants against the damage of strong light [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gould KS. Natures Swiss army knife: the diverse protective roles of anthocyanins in leaves. J Biomed Biotechnol. 2004;2004:31420." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1592">39</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li S, Wang W, Gao J, Yin K, Wang R, Wang C, Petersen M, Mundy J, Qiu JL. MYB75 phosphorylation by MPK4 is required for light-induced anthocyanin accumulation in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 2016;28:286683." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1595">40</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>b).</p><p>During the fruit ripening, the fruit peel color of some varieties turns from green to yellow, a process called degreening. This is virtually a process of chlorophyll degradation mediated by Chl catabolic genes (CCGs) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhu X, Chen J, Qiu K, Kuai B. Phytohormone and light regulation of chlorophyll degradation. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:1911." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1604">41</a>]. Hongyu is a typical variety whose fruits experience degreening process and exhibit yellow-peel, while Guire82 is a Chinese cultivar derived from yellow-peel Neelum and represents atypical stay-green peel (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>a). No major differences in the expression of CCGs were detected between the green-peel Guire82 and the yellow-peel Hongyu during ripening (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig4">4</a>c).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Mango germplasm diversity</h3><p>We selected 48 <i>M. indica</i> accessions and 4 additional species of <i>Mangifera</i> for whole-genome resequencing, including 35 typical cultivars representing the history of mango breeding in different areas of the globe and 13 landraces germplasms collected from remote areas in South China. Across the 52 accessions plus the variety Alphonso, a total of 21.04 million high-confidence variants, or 53.9 variants per kilobyte in average, were identified. They include 19,433,034 SNPs, 635,320 insertions, and 972,376 deletions; 4,297,808 variants (20.4%) were located in genic regions, including 542,626 synonymous, 828,252 nonsynonymous, and 2,849,557 intronic variants. Among them, 69.1% have a minor allele frequency of less than 10%. This tendency was even greater for functional variants, as the frequencies of nonsense mutation sites were 0.15% with MAF &lt;10%, while its value was decreased to 0.03% with MAF &gt;90% (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM25">25</a>: Table S23).</p><p>As expected, all the mango varieties form a group distinct from the four other <i>Mangifera</i> species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>a, b). Phylogenetic inference splits the mango varieties into two distinct groups, with indigenous varieties from Southeast Asia residing in one group and traditional varieties from India in the other. This coincides with the long-proposed two centers of domestication, one in India and the other in continental Southeast Asia [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schnell RJ, Olano CT, Meerow AW, Campbell RJ, Kuhm DN. Mango genetic diversity analysis and pedigree inferences for Florida cultivars using microsatellite. J AMER Soc HORT SCI. 2006;13:11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1639">42</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="RJ. BJaS: The mango: botany, production and uses. In The mango: botany, production and uses. Edited by RE L. Wallingford: CAB International; 1997: 27." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1642">43</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>b). The overall <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> between <i>Mangifera</i> wild relatives and Southeast Asia/India varieties (0.1747/0.1856) is higher than that between Southeast Asia and India varieties (0.1358). Furthermore, the USA varieties are more closely related to India varieties than to Southeast Asia varieties, agreeing with previous analysis using 25 microsatellite loci [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schnell RJ, Olano CT, Meerow AW, Campbell RJ, Kuhm DN. Mango genetic diversity analysis and pedigree inferences for Florida cultivars using microsatellite. J AMER Soc HORT SCI. 2006;13:11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6606e1656">42</a>] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>b, c). Landraces collected in South China were clustered together with South East germplasms (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>b), some of which experienced allelic admixture (<i>k</i>=3, 4; Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>c). Although grouped with India germplasms, most of the commercial varieties experienced allelic admixture (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>c). India and Southeast Asia varieties have a comparable level of genetic diversity (<i></i>) (0.0084 and 0.0094) as that of other <i>Mangifera</i> species (0.0094). The regions of high <i></i> values in the mango genome coincide with highly repetitive areas (i, iiaiic; Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#Fig5">5</a>d). No significantly enriched functional roles were observed in genetic diversity-declined regions compared between <i>Mangifera</i> wild relatives and India type/Southeast Asia type with different parameters.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-01959-8/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1959_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1022" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Genomic diversity of <i>M. indica</i> varieties and relatives within <i>Mangifera</i>. 49 <i>M. indica</i> germplasms and 4 otherspecies in the genus of <i>Mangifera</i> were sampled for the analyses. (<b>a</b>) PCA analysis of the samples using SNP markers. The three groups indicated by phylogenetic (<b>b</b>) and STRUCTURE (<i>k</i>=3) (<b>c</b>) analyses were circled, respectively. Germplasms with different backgrounds are represented with dots with different shapes and colors. (<b>b</b>) Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of the samples in polar layout based on SNPs. Clades of the three major groups are indicated with different colors as indicated in STRUCTURE analysis (<i>k</i>=3). Tips of outgroup germplasms are indicated with green hollow blocks. Tips of traditional and commercial varieties are labeled with filled rounds, and those representing landraces are suggested as hollow circular forms. Traditional varieties and commercial cultivars without admixture are labeled in red, and germplasms representing Southeast Asia varieties and landraces are in yellow, while those with allele admixture are in blue. (<b>c</b>) STRUCTURE analysis of the samples, with each color representing one population, and the length of each color segment in each vertical bar representing the proportion contributed by ancestral populations. On the right is assumed number of clusters (<i>k</i>), and below is the name or origin of the samples. (<b>d</b>) Circos demonstration of genetic diversity. Outer circle represents 20 pseudo-molecules of mango genome. i, contents of repetitive elements; iia- iic, nucleotide diversity () of other <i>Mangifera</i> species, varieties of Southeast Asia and India germplasms, respectively. iiia-iiic, population differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) levels of other <i>Mangifera</i> species vs. germplasms of Southeast Asia, other <i>Mangifera</i> species vs. India germplasms, and those of Southeast Asia vs. India, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Despite the widespread admixture, inbreeding played important roles in the mango breeding history. The values of the inbreeding coefficient and cumulative length of runs of homozygosity (ROH) varied within different cultivars (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM26">26</a>: Table S24 and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S10). As expected, the admixture of both the Southeast Asia and India germplasms have lower inbreeding coefficient and small amounts of ROH. Interestingly, we found the length of ROH was related to genomic heterozygous rates in mango cultivars (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-01959-8#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S11). The Southeast Asia germplasms have a higher inbreeding coefficient and have more regions of runs of homozygosity (ROH). Three India and USA germplasms close to the variety Alphonso have the largest inbreeding coefficient. The excess of ROH by inbreeding is the reason why Alphonso have a lower level of heterozygosity. The USA cultivar Irwin also has larger regions of ROH, suggesting the existence of inbreeding in Irwin breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>A high-quality reference genome for mangoes can facilitate mango molecular breeding and evolutionary studies of Anacardiaceae.
